THE ladies and gentlemen of the Alabama Press Association were tfcÂ«s
guests of The Call yesterday on a trip around" the bay on board the tug
Defiance. Every minute of the time from 9:30 a. m. to 1:30 p. m. was en-
Joyed by the agreeable and accomplished visitors"frbm"Alabama. Captain
Joseph F. Trewren. commander of the tug," took'excelleht'care of his pas-
Fengers, and not the slightest mishap occurred to Interfere with the day's
enjoyment. The atmosphere was slightly hazy, but the temperature was delightful
and all nthc-r conditions were favorable for an* outing 'on the "water. Â¦ The -tug
steamed slowly away from Pacific-street wharf, pausing a proper distance from
the pier to give the photographers a good range for taking views of the decorated
'.raft. The course from the start was ocean-ward. ' Interesting places on the shore.
Black Point and Presidio reservations, Fort Point. Lime Point ! and . the Cliff
Hovse, were pointed out to the excursionists. Captain Trewren. mindful of the
ladies and appreciating: the store of tempting refreshments, solid and liquid.
which the caterers had placed aboard in the early morning, did not plunge
ahead or venture too far west of Fort Point. The splashes of spray and the roil
grave the Alabamans a slight acquaintance with the Pacific Ocean. The tug re
turning steamed close to the Marin shore. The orchestra aboard discoursed ' en
chanting music, while the scenic views of Sausalito and Belvedere refreshed the
eyer. of the guests. . - .
After passing through Raccoon Straits the tug paused in the smooth water un
der the lee of Angel Island. There the vessel came to a Â¦ slow down and rest to
give the guild of the fourth estate opportunity, to discuss the wines, , fruits and
sa!ads of California and such other articles as the hospitality of the Golden "West
provided for the visttprs. The busy caterers, assisted by \ several ladles of the
local fraternity, kept pace with the demands of the hour. , The orchestra played
from olives and bo! ads to meats, fruits, nuts and raisins. . A strong sentiment was
created among the visitors in favor of cruising off Angel Island for an indefinite
length of time, but the desire to see more of thÂ» Bay of San Francisco and to get
& near view of the historic battleship Oregon overcame other emotions. -
The photographers, professional and of the amateur grade, got in their work
after the lunch hour. Groups of lovely ladies were detached from the main
throngs aft, forward and on the pilot-house deck to lend the charm of. Southern
beauty and grace to the picture collections of the cruise. . . Â¦ Â¦ , Â¦
Running away from Angel Island Captain Trewren brought his boat so. near
the famous battleship Oregon that the excursionists and the sailor men exchanged
salutations. â¢ . â¢ â¢â¢'
The orchestra aboard the tug played the "Star Spangled Banner" and the audi
ence joined In Blnging the words of the national air. There was a universal de
sire expressed by the ladies and gentlemen of the Alabama party to go .aboard the
Oregon, and If the committees of the California Press Association and theÂ» San
Francisco Press Club having the entertaining of the visitors in charge will ar
range some hour for the editors and publishers to visit the ship and 6ee the guns
and turrets made memorable in American history, they will receive the unani
mous thanks of the Southern delegation. In any case. The Call
will try to arrange for such a trip. All the signs aboard the battleship, the
cheers and signals In response to the greetings from the tug signified that the
officers and others would be glad to receive the Alabama party. It is pleasant
to reflect that the distinguished families of the South are. again largely repre
sented in the army and navy of the united country and no one can entertain
greater pride in the achievements of the army and navy than that 'entertained by
those patriotic editors of Alabama and their captivating companions. Every ship
of the navy and every regiment of the army contains "boys" of .the South, and
many of them are Alabama "boys." And then the officers of the Oregon would
be enraptured by the eweet Southern way of dwelling lightly on ! the "r." The
Oregon was not the only ship of the country noticed. - The cutter Hugh McCul-
Icch that served as a dispatch boat between Manila and Hongkong, and which en r
tered the harbor of Manila when Dewey's cruisers went in to smash 'the Spanish
fleet engaged the attention of the visitors.
ThÂ» Union Iron Works, wh^-e the Ohio Is In progress of construction, did not
pet the overlook.' The tug Bteamed along to Hunters Point and then came about
for the run home. The guests from Alabama were impressed with the magnitude
of San Francisco, but what impressed them most was the. hospitality of Califor
nia. They epoke of the kindness shown to them by the citizens of Stockton and
Fresno. After reviewing the excursion on the bay tendered by The Call and specu
lating* on scheduled features of entertainments to come, they reached the happy
and complimentary verdict that Calif ornians were *just like Southerners in the
generous extension of hospitality. "We never expected to- meet such, kindness.
You are Just like our people in Alabama," exclaimed a lady who found special de
light in the trip. . , . -, : â¢

On the other hand, the official returns show that in the various fights the For
ty-third killed 1693 Filipinos, wounded 325 and captured 1426 of the rebels. The reg
iment lost three h,orses, three rifles' and three ammunition belts, while it captured
from the enemy ninety-seven rifles, forty-two Â¦ cannon, 3557 rounds of ammunition
and $27,900 In Mexican money. The regiment was In 293 engagements in which
there were casualties and sixty-one engagements In which no casualties occurred.'
Two companies, L and M. of the Forty-third were recruited in California and
sailed from here in November, 1S99. The other companies were recruited f rom^ all
over the United States and sailed from New York on November 16, 1899. The entlrÂ©
regiment landed'ln Manila on January 1. 1900, and was at once sent to garrison
the Islands of ,Saraar and Leytc. As Samar is the third and Leyte the fifth In Rise
in the Philippines, the amount of work the men had to do can be imagined. At
first eight companies were stationed on Samar and four on Leyte, but later two
companies were sent from Samar to Leyte. â¢ Still later another regiment was sent
to Samar and the companies of the Forty-third doing duty there were sent to
Leyte. On May 29 last General Moxlca and all his men surrendered, and on June 1
the regiment sailed for Manila and four days later started for home.

Colonel Arthur Murrey is very popular with his men and a splendid commander.
It wasldue to his disposition of his men that such excellent results were produced.
In order to garrison two islands there had to be a great deal of scattering, but
each little command was so placed that it could speedily call upon its neighbor for
assistance. It was due" to this fact that every officer in the. regiment got a*
chance to do some fighting on his own account. Colonel Murrey Is proud of his
regiment and its record shows that he la deservedly so. \
Â¦ Lieutenant Charles C. , Estes, who was . in forty-four engagements, has men
from, the ranks. -From 1S84 to 1S89 he wai private, corporal, sergeant and first ser
geant in the Third Artillery., In 189*? he was a. captain in the Eighth Volunteer In
fantry and, was mustered out In March. 1S39. On November 2, 1S99, he enlisted as a
private in the Forty-third and two weeks later he was: made a -second lieutenant.
The warmest engagement In which Estes took part was on Easter Sunday, April
15; 1S99. With/twenty-four men he was holding the village of Jaro on the island
'of ; Leyte. .'The. men were quartered in tha village church and everybody save the
sentry was asleep." At 4 a. m. over 1000 Filipinos appeared before the church and
.regiment lost two officersand sixty-seven men In battle. The' officers
were Lieutenants John H. Evans and Jonathan Cilley. Of the men, one was hanged
by the rebels in* Samar, one .was. captured and beheaded in Leyte. one was cap
tured andÂ«beheaded In. Samar. one -was drowned, six died from bolo wounds and the
others from various causes. ' - / . â¢ â¢ . . ,1 V

The funeral of Louis A. Garnett, the ex
pert on matters of finance' and currency
and former manager of what Is now the
Selby Smelting Works, was held yester
day at Laurel Hill Cemetery. Many
friends of deceased and his family attend
ed the services and the Interment.
Mr. Garnetfs death occurred Tuesday
night at Pacific Grove, where he and Mrs.
Garnett had been living for a year past.
Apoplexy was the cause of death. - -
Louis A. Garnett was v born in iVIrginla
In 1822 and was graduated from the Vir
ginia Military Institute in ,1842. He came
to California in 1850, and from 1857 to 1860
was metallurgical ofllcer in the Mint. He
resigrned that position to go to Europe,
where he spent several years in the study
of finance and economics. After his re
turn to San Francisco he organised the
San Francisco Assaying and Refining
Works, now the Selby Smelting Works,
which he managed until 3876.
It was at the suggestion of Mr. Garnett
that the Mint Bureau at Washington was
organized by the mint act of 1873. provid
ing for the direction of all the mints from
Washington instead of from the Phila
delphia Mint. Mr. Garnett was recog
nized as the highest authority on mone
tary questions on the coast. He served
as a member of the United States Mone-'
tary Commission of 1S97, and his col
leagues relied largely upon his. technical
knowledge in framing Its recommenda
tions, r .'

EL PASO, Tex., June 27.â P. D. Cun
ningham, chief of the, United States
Boundary Commission Â¦; and commandant
of the fleet of the United States and Mex
ico which left here last month to' explore
the grand canyon of the Rio Grande, was
brought back yesterday in a serious con
dition from the effects of a snake bite.
The reptile, a huge water moccasin,
crawled over him while he slept. He
sprang up, but the snake buried its fangs
In his foot. A comrade sucked the poison
from the wound and Cunninsrham was
sent here for medical treatment.

Major Humphreys went from here to
China in command of the Third Artillery.
After the relief of Peking he went to
Manila and was placed in command of
Fort Banyan. He was ordered home last
month and Is now on his way to Fort Ste
vens, Oregon, to assume command of that
post.

LAST OF VOLUNTEERS HERE.
Away back in the hills Sweeney heard
of the peril of l:is command. The rescue
party Joined with his own men only made
a grand total of seventeen men, while the
rebels had been reinforced and must have
been 150D strong. Securing the launch
Laoang, Sweeney and his men raced down
stream to the rescue of their comrades.
Reaching Catutig the handful of men
jumped aehore and charged the Filipino
army. Thinking others were coming, the
rebels broke and fled. Lieutenant Sweeney
then retreated, taking with him the eleven
men he had rescued, and finally reached
the headquarters of the regiment.
Many of the officers of the Forty-third
trere made very happy by letters from the
War Department handed to them as soon
as the mail was opened. The letters con
tained the appointment- of the following
to the regular army: Captainsâ Golds
borougrh. Beavers. Duncan (of California).
Tilton, Hansen and Fair; first lieutenants
âDonnelly. Seaman, Sterrett, Burt. Phll
lpps, Swann and uasser; second lieuten
antsâJohnstone, Steele and Andrews. Be
sides these there were one or two others
whose names could not be learned. Among
them, however, was that of Sergeant Ma
jcr Edward K. Masisie, who has been ap
pointed a second lieutenant.
Lieutenant Sweeney's Daring.
When Lieutenant Sweeney started
across the island he left thirty-one men
in garrison at Catubig. He was not gone
.very long before General Lukbanand 1000
rebels appeared before the place and de
manded the surrender of the men. The an
fwer was a volley. The fighting went on
day after day, the Americans being picked
oft one at a time until only eleven were
left. Still there was no thought of sur
render, and for every American that fell
twenty Filipinos bit the dust.
The Filipinos had two cannon, which
they could not bring Into action owing to
the sharpshooting of the Americans. After
the fight the bodies of nineteen rebels were
found beside oae of the guns. The Fili
pinos attacked again and again with great
determination, but were finally driven off,
leaving 125 dead, one rifle, two cannon, 150
bolos and a silk flag- on the field.
On March 16 last Estes and his orderly
were ambushed by 150 Filipinos. The or
derly was killed but Estes cut his way
through the horde and escaped. .
Major W. E. "Wilder of the Fourth Cav
alry was also lieutenant colonel of the
Forty-third for a time. He was with Gen
eral Toung in Northern Luzon. When
Major Batson was wounded Major Wilder
was placed In command of the Macabebe
scouts. On October 1. 1900. he was made
6uperintendent of police in Manila and
served until May' 27, 190L Major Wilder
cays Manila is one of the best policed
cities In the world and has a splendid de
tective bureau, under the direction of
Philip K. Sweet, formerly of the Forty-
Eixth Infantry.
Lieutenant A. E. Phillips was In com
mand In some very hot affairs and was
one of the officers present at the surren
der of General Moxlca and the consequent
pacification of Leyte. â¢
Lieutenant Joseph T.Â« Sweeney Is still
Buffering from the effects of his wounds.
With twelve men he was marching across
Samar when the rebels ambushed him In
the hills. Two of his comrades were killed
and two wounded. For five days the hand
ful of soldiers kept the Filipinos at bay
and then help came.
Filipino Attack Is Repulsed.

Estes and his men. The Americans, in
their night clothes, seized their arms and
for five hours fought the rebels, finally
driving- them off. Lieutenant Estes with
six men guarded one side of the bell
tower, while Sergeant Rich and four men

under orders to proceed to the United
States. They will be sent home on the

paring discharges for the men and mak

commence at 7 a. m. and will continue
until every man of the seven regiments
has received his travel pay, savings and
balance due from his clothing account.

Nevada Mining Property Sold.
GRASS VALLEY. June 27.â A telegram
from Hawthorne. Nev., states that O. A
Turner of this city has completed the
purchase of the mining properties owned
by J. F. Butler at Tonojah, the purchase
price being $338,000. Associated with
Turner Is John A. Anderson, a Philadel
phia capitalist. Of the purchase price
$50,000 has already been paid, $100,000 to
be paid in ninety days, and $186,000 before
January L The new owners will not
take possession before the first of next
year.

LOS ANGELES, June 27.â A- decision
was handed don-n this morning by Judge
Noyes of Uie Riverside .County Superior
Court, sitting for Judge Shaw of the Su
perior Court of Los Angeles County, de
claring the water bonds issued- by the
city of Los Angeles invalid.
The aggregate, value of the bonds is $2,
090,000, and they were issued for thÂ© pur
chase of the Improvements of the Los An
geles City Water Company. None of the
bonds have been sold on account of the
question of their legality. The action in
volving their legality and decided this
morning by Judge Noyes was brought by
Henry D. Barrows, a taxpayer of Los An
geles, to set aside the proceedings leading
up to the issue cf the bonds. Judge Noyes
In the course of his decision said:
The proceedings leading up to the Issue of
these bonds were begun under a general latr
regulating the procedure for the issue of bonds,
but before said bonds were It-sued or sold the
Legislature passed an act entitled "an act
authorizing the Incurring of Indebtedness by
cities, towns and municipal corporations for
municipal Improvements, regulating the acqui
sition, construction and completion thereof."
This act became a law under the constitutional
provision without the Governor's approval. Feb
ruary 25. 1901, and the same Is now the law
of the State governing the Issue of bonds o!
municipal corporations. '
Had these bonds been voted, siyned. sold and
issued In accordance with the law under which
the proceedings for their issue were originally
commenced, there could, of course. bÂ« no ques
tion as to their validity In so far as this onÂ»
question Is concerned, but. some of the most
important proceedings are yet to bÂ« had before
they can become available as assets of the city
for the purpose for which they were voted. The
question therefore, presents itself: Does the act
of February 2S. 1901. now control In the sale and
Issue of municipal bonds? If such be the case,
does not such act supersede the general aet
under which the said bonds were issued and
must not all proceedings be had under the act
of February 25, 1901?

SANTA CRUZ, June 27!â The Supreme
Court has decided the suit of the county
of Santa Cruz against. McPherson &"Wal
dron, proprietors of the Sentinel, in favor
fft the defendants. The case is of interest
to all publishers, as, it virtually settles
the question as to the number of times a
delinquent tax list should be published.
The law states that the list must be pub
lished once a week for three consecutive
weeks.
Acting- upon the advice of attorneys, the
defendants published the list four times
in 1S95 and the bill was allowed by the
Supervisors.. Subsequently the county
brought suit to recover $1035 25 on the
ground that the publication the fourth
time was illegal and therefore an over
charge.
Judge Doollnp of Hollister sustained a
demurrer, from which the county took
an appeal. The court in Its decision sus
taining Doolins says: . â
"The action of the Board of Supervisors
in awarding a contract for the printing
of the delinquent list and in subsequently
allowing- the claim therefor under the cir
cumstances disclosed by the complaint is
conclusive Â¦ of the matter and in face of
such action it cannot be said that the
money was paid to the defendants without
authority of law.
"The order of the board duly made con
stituted 'authority of law.' The Board of
Supervisors was the proper tribunal be
fore which to make objection that the
claim for printing was excessive by rea
son of fraudulent conspiracy or for any
other reason, and the board having taken
due action in the matter all objections
that might have been made to the claim
before it are precluded." ' .
Supreme Tribunal 'Passes', on
Delinquent Tax List
Affairs.

TO RULE WORLD
Special Dispatch to ThÂ« CalL
CHICAGO. June 27.â In 1M0 France inn
ported only 16,152,000 bushels of wheat- Of
this amount the United States supplied
less than 1,500,000 bus*hels, but exports
from Argentine Republic were nearly
double those of former years. Russia "also
had a good crop and exports were heavy.
This year It is estimated that Russia will
export less than In 1900, and Argentine
20,000,000 bushels less.
That being true, the United States will
probably supply the bulk of France's
wheat deficit. Jt is hard to approximate
the cost and profit of such a movement.
In 1&00 the average export price for the
United States crop was 72 cents a bushel,
but the total crop of this country amount
ed to only 522.229,505 bushels. This year
the estimates are that the total crop of
this country will be 700,000,000. Granting
that the increased demand abroad win
maintain the price at 72 cents, the bill for
supplying the total French, wheat deficit
Â¦would be $25,923,000, asuming the amount
of that deficit to be 36,000,000 bushels. Just
what exact amount the United States will
supply it is impossible to estimate cor
rectly.
F. G. Ix)gan, head of one of the largest
commission houses in Chicago, said to
night:
"Estimates which I have obtained place
the figures as high as G0,000,000 bushels.
Only the United States can supply this
deficit. The winter crop of wheat is now
made, and the prospects for the spring
crop are excellent. A conservative esti
mate places our yield fcr the year at
750 OOO.WX) buahels. The average for recent
vears was only 500.000,000 bushels. This
country yearly exports 200,000,000 bushels
of wheat. It Is easy to see, therefore,
that we can supply France's deficit with
out feeling It. Russia's yield of wheat
has been declining durin*" the last few
vears, and the same may be said with re
gard to certain countries of South.Amer
ica. Confcequently the prospect for the
United States to come to the front and
assert her commercial and agricultural su
premacy was never better than It is now."
BOERS CAPTURE
FIVE SANGAR5
Special Dlsnatch to The. Call.
CAPE TOWN, June 27.â Boer invaders,
under Malan and Smit, about 3000 strong,
attacked Richmond early Tuesday morn
ing. The town was defended by a de
tachment of the North Staffordshire Mi
litia under Captain Hawkshaw and ths
town guard.. Three sangars outside the
town were captured by the Boers, the
garrison being distributed principally at
the Jail fort and the Flagstaff Hill fort.
Owing to the capture of the sangars,' the
Flagstaff Hill fort was exposed to a
heavy fire.
Three times during the day Command
ant Malan sent messengers demanding
the surrender of the town, and each time
the demand was refused. The Boers took
advantage of an opportunity during the
cessation of the hostilities to better their
posltigns and on the last occasion when
the messenger was sent In a party of
burghers entered the town and effected
the capture of the sangar In the market
square. Not a shot was fired on account
of the white flag carried by the mes
senger.
The Boers, who were now only 200 yards
from the Jail fort, were securely covered
and directed a continuous flre for two
hours upon the defenders, who stoutly
held their position. Finding it imposslb'e
to capture the jail fort, the Boers turned
their attention to another sangar in the
town, held by eight men. These eight
men, after having held the position *oi
eleven hours, surrendered at 5 o'clock in
the afternoon. . . â¢ Â¦
After dark the British garrison
strengthened the fortifications, as they
anticipated a renewal or the attack in the
morning. During the night, however, the
Boers learned that relief was approach
ing for the besieged men and, withdrew.
They captured twelve horses and looted
the stores.-
The engagement lasted twelve hour;-.
The Boer loss is reported as five men
killed and seven wounded. The British
casualties are not given. On Wednesday
morning Colonel Lunds, with the relieving
column, entered the town.
I am of the opinion that the property cap
tured on the land was not intended to be made
a maritime prize, and that It Is excluded by
the tej-ms of the act from which the right is
sought to be derived, and that the libellant Is
not entitled to an adjudication In prize of the
appraised value of such property appropriated
by the United States. This renders unnecessary
the consideration of the effect of . the treaty
with Spain upon the rights of â¢ the libellant.
The next question Â¦ is whether the libellant is
entitled to the condemnation in prize of the
value of the property taken from the vessels
sunk or otherwise destroyed. It is objected
that inasmuch as the libellant has received
bounty for the destruction of these vessels he
is not entitled as prize for the -property taken
from them; that bounty la a substitute for the
prize itself, something given, "in lieu of it."
Bounty Is not an equivalent for prize." There
is, therefore, / no reason / arising out of the
amount paid as bounty that would Justify the
claim that, in fairness such reward should be
exclusive. I find nothing to prohibit the con
demnation In prize of : the ' property captured
or. taken from the ships sunk or destroyed, In
addition to- the bounty paid for the destruction
of the ship, and I am of opinion that It
should be 1 allowed. . . -. '. ** . ..â¢ . .
That Rear Admiral Sampson and the of
ficers and men under his command are en
titled to one-half the value of the Infanta
Maria Teresa,' which was raised and sub
sequently abandoned; the Reina Mercedes,
Sandoval and Alvarado and the' muni
tions of war and the other property taken
from the vessels they destroyed.
The court declared that the property
captured on the land by the naval force
operating from the sea Is not a lawful
prize. This point has never been passed
upon by the Supreme Court, and an ap
peal may be taken. On this point Judge
Bradley says:
That Admiral Dewey 'and the offlcers
and men under his' command are entitled
to one-half the value of all the vessels
sunk in the battle of Manila Bay and sub
sequently raised; of the guns, the mu
nitions of war and, other property taken
from the Spanish vesels which were sunk
in the engagement. . They are not entitled,
however, to prize money for the property
captured on the shore or for the cascos
and other harbor vessels taken subsequent
to the engagement. ' . ;v;
This is the decision of Judge Bradley
of the Equity Court of the District of Co
lumbia. Briefly, the court held: **

fleets of Admirals Montejo and Cervera at
Manila and Santiago, Admiral Dewey and

ers will be allowed prize money. Â¦ /

During the afternoon an Informal meet
ing, of directors of Important banks was
held at a downtown -club and the suspen
sion of the Seventh National and its rela
tions with other financial institutions 'was
thoroughly discussed. A tacit-understand
ing that all Interests should be protected
was arrived at. ' .'.>'Â¦.- â¢
Edward Thomas, president of the Seventh
National Bank, stated that the difficulties of
the bank were due solely to the making- by the
previous executive officers of large advances to
Marquand & Co., and that the security thereon
was of an unmarketable character, although of
considerable intrinsic value. That upon the
urgent application of the board, he and his as
sociate, Edwin II. Gould, had furnished finan
cial assistance . upon adequate securities and
upon the assurance that the obligations of Mar
quand & - Co. would be made good by this
morning. That the firm named had however
failed to fulfill expectations and while he and
Mr. Gould and some of the directors were will
ing to furnish further financial aid, It was not
deemed by them, as Just either to the deposi
tors, stockholders or the public to invite de
posits and credits until the affairs of the batik
were again' placed upon an absolutely safe ba
sis. That for this purpose reasonable time was
necessary, and to prevent' unfaiir i preference or
advantages, the board determined, with the ad
vice of counsel, to suspend operations for the
present.
The Associated Press issued no such
bulletin nor carried any such bulletin Jike
it in statement or purport.

added that he was engaged in preparing a

For two days under the watchful eyes

have been made. On Tuesday the bank

Clearing-house they had decided to sus
pend.
The Marquand loan was incurred within
the period of a. month, and is secured by
collateral of which one bank president
said to-day: "I would not be found dead
with such collateral in my, bank."
The loan is secured among other things
by some $G00,000 or $700,000' of bonds of
the Pittsburg, Shawmut and' Northern
road and "sweetened" with Hackensack
Land and Improvement Company securi
ties, stock of the Marquand building in
Broadway and stocks and bonds of the
Gas and Electric Company of Bergen
County, New Jersey.

was simply unfortunate. Besides that It

Taking in the situation at a glance, the
pugilist ran across the street to intercept
the horses. As they passed him in their
mad career he jumped for a rein that was
hanging loose from the horse nearest him.
Holding on with all his might and brac
ing himself against the railroad track3
that line the avenue, he drew back. The
effect of this movement, which was all
done in a good deal less time than It
takes to tell it, was to throw the horse on
its . hauncher and to bring both to a
standstill, where- they snorted and ca
reened to no purpose.

Attorney Frank Powers notified Acting
Auditor Wells yesterday that he would
enjoin the. payment of the. claims of
teachers for . November and December;
1898, unless they agreed to return the sum
of $7000 , which, was surrendered under an
a greement by " merchant ' creditors in May, 1
1899.'- At that time about $27,000 was de
posited , with the Union Trust Company'
pending the outcome of litigation to-de
termine-- whether the teachers, or mer
chants were entitled to the money. -^ A"
compromise.- w as * afterward effected,
whereby the merchants released the sum;
of 57000 tothe teachers, who received a
small percentage of their Individual sal
ary demands, less about $400 spent as at-,
torney's fees. It is this $7000 that Powers
wants returned to the merchants/ Â¦
Acting Auditor Wells declined to hold
out the sum named, as he thinks the,dis
pute is a mattef to be settled between the
teachers and/ merchants. Powers there
upon expressed his intention to sue out a
writ of injunction, to restrain the Auditor
from paying the teachers the full amount
of the balance due them until the mer
chants are paid the $7000. The agreement
under which the suit will be brought fol
lows: :
The merchant creditors having claims against
the school fund of 1898-1899, which the Union
Trust Company holds as their assignee, shall
execute and deliver to the Auditor and to the
Teachers' Committee a written agreement to
the effect that the . merchant ' creditors and
the. Union Trust Company, agree that none of
their unpaid demands shall be audited or paid
until all of the salary demands of teachers and
janitors for November, 1898, shall have been
audited and there shall have come into the
fechool fund of 1898-1899 a sufficient sum to pay
all- of the demands for November, 189S, the
Union Trust Company ' to pay Auditor "Wells
the : sum of $7000, to be divided among the
teachers, signing the agreement. -

of Teachers* Back
Claims.

Burglars who. yesterday morning broke
into the saloon of C. Eastman, at the cor
ner of Mason and Ellis streets, en
deavored to hide their crime' by deliber
ately setting fire to the premises.
i When Proprietor Eastman closed the sa
loon at 2 in the morning he carefully
locked the side and front doors and went
*to his residence at the corner of Turk
and Jones streets. .. .
At 4:30 yesterday morning Police Of
ficer Harry Hook and Special Officer Mas
sey were walking along Ellis street and
tried the side door of Eastman's saloon.
The door yielded to their touch and when
it opened a volume of smoke poured out
into the street.
Hook turned in a fire alarm and with
Massey dashed into the saloon and put
out the flames that were licking the walls
of the premises. In the rear of the sa
loon where two "private ' boxes" were lo
cated, the ; officers discovered three-dis
tinct" fires. Heaps of oil-saturated paper
were lying on the floor. The burglars had
set fire to the paper in the hope of de
stroying the saloon and wiping out all
Â¦traces of robbery. . -
The officers took buckets of Â¦ water and
before the fire apparatus arrived suc
ceeded ' in Quenching the flames that
threatened to destroy the building.
The proprietor of the saloon was called
to the scene by , Willis Davis, who for-:
merly worked as a bartender for East
man. Eastman informed the police of
ficers that when he left his saloon he had
carefully locked the place., The front
door is fastened with a Yale lock, whilo
the side door has only an ordinary lock
that could be opened with a skeleton key.
. Fire Marshal Towe questioned Eastman
and .was satisfied that he had no knowl
edge of the cause of the fire. Detectives
Wren and DInan were placed on the case
by Chief of Detectives Seymour and they
learned that a number of hoodlums had
been .In the habit of frequenting East
man's = saloon and had been refused free
drinks and loans of moneys .Eastman in
formed the detectives that he suspected
his saloon had been set on fire by some
of these hoodlums, and that he especially
suspected a certain stable boy. ' Investiga
tion by â¢ Detectives Wren and Dinan
showed that -a drawer containing $8 in
cash had been rifled and a slot machine
opened by. means of. a burglar's ','jimmy."

SAN FRANCISCO.
San Francisco 6, Sacramento O.
A strange game and a good one was
played at 'Recreation Grounds -yester
day afternoon, when the Senators met the
Wasps in. the initial hostilities of the se
ries. At the outset the pretzel destroyers
fell upon Sapho Harper's curves for four
hits, which resulted in five runs. Then
Harper settled like stale beer and was
undiscoverable ever after. Iburg twirled
for the home team, and despite the eight
hits made off his delivery not a single
Senator crossed the bags for a run.
â¢ The Senators : were bombarded with ill
luck yesterday, jj On several, occasions the
smell of the rubber on the return journey
hit; their nostrils, but the scorer rang no
bell for Sacramento. The score:
Harper Was Found at Outset
but Settled in Surpris
ing Fashion. .
Bunch of Hits in. First
Inning Gives Frisco
the Game.

William Henry," a cooper, who has a
wife and two young children, appeared in
Judge Mogan's court yesterday : on a
charge of disturbing the peace. '.He- was
arrested Wednesday afternoon " on" Sixth
street by Policeman Fontana, who had
been notified by several 'ladles* that a man
had been grabbing hold of them and mak
ing: insulting proposals to them. "\ ' '
Fontana happened to be on Sixth street;
between Market and Mission, ;. and
saw a man grab hold of Miss -Mary
Cleary, 14 Morris avenue, whom he knew.
Suspecting 'he was the man complained
of, the policeman asked him what he was
doing. . Henry replied that the lady was
his landlady, Mrs. Livingston of Howard
street, and that he had simply accosted
her. Fontana . placed him under arrest
and Â»he threatened the^. officer,, alleging
that he was making a serious mistake. ;
Miss Cleary was. in court yesterday
morning and told the Judge that Henry
struck her on the shoulder when she re
sisted him, and the Judge ordered'that
another charge of battery be booked
against him.. It was done and the case
was . continued until to-morrow. Henry's
only excuse was that he was , drunk. : â¢

Scatters of Interest to Mariners and

The Admiral u-111 load railroad ties at Noyo
for Guayaquil (former charter canceled),
The Madrono could not lift the Iowa's
anchor yesterday, so a diver was sent
down to see what was the matter. On her
last trip the Thomas lost an anchor in the
same spot, and'the battleship's mudhook
and chain has got mixed up with it. A
couple of tugs assisting the Slocum will
be required to get the immense mass of
iron up.
The big freight steamer Hawaiian sailed
for Honolulu, Kahului and Hllo yester
day.
The tramp steamship Horda with 2,009,
000 feet of railroad ties will get away for
Guayaquil next Sunday. She will be fol
lowed by the Victoria. The latter steamer
is now on the Sound, but on her return to
San Francsico she will be sent to Noyo
to load for Ecuador.
Jess Reed was arrested by a deputy
sheriff from Sonoma as soon as the steam
ship Alliance arrived yesterday morning.
He is wanted in Santa Rosa for burglary.
From Santa Rosa he made his way to
Marshfield, Or., and then came here, only
to be arrested on the wharf.
NEWS OF THE OCEAN.
"Water Front Notes.
The Buford and Kilpatrlck are both go
ing to New Tork via. Manila. The Buford
will get away about July 10, and on her
arrival in the East will be put in the
Cuban trade. The Kilpatrick will get
away about a week after the Buford.
The Logan and Thomas are being got
ready to carry the school teachers to the
Philippines. The Logan will get away
about July 5 with 125 female and 300 male
teachers. The Thomas will get away a
few days later, while the Grant, with
army officers and civilians, will sail about
July 15.
Movements of Transports.
pÂ»Â»â j HE British schooner Alexander ar-
I rived In port Wednesday night in
I distress. She was chartered here
j| about elx weelcs ago to take a gen
eral cargo of merchandise and' a
deckloadof lumber to Petropaulovsky. Si
beria. After the merchandise had been
put aboard she went to Ofays Harbor and
there took on ter decWoad of lumber. A
start was then made for the Siberian
coast- This -was on the 13th inst-, and all.
â¢went well for a time. Then It came on to
blow a gale-from the northwest and the
schooner was hove to. She labored a great
deal, and when in latitude 45 degTees
north, longitude 133 degrees west, sprung
a leak. The men worked on the pumps for
a couple of days, but when the captain
found the water was gaining he decided
to run for San Francisco for repairs. The
chances are that the Alexander's cargo
will be discharged and the vessel put on
the drydock.
Captain Lockluaxt Heard From.
About *Us months *go Captain Lock
hart and H. Olsen of the Pacific Coast
Steamship Company. gave up their jobs
and prepared for a voyage to Nome. Over
at Sausallto they built a small schooner,
which they named the Helen. She was
provisioned, for two years, and on June 12
the adventurous pair, accompanied by two
sailors, started for St. Michael. When two
days out they ran into the northwester,
and soon, the seams opening up, the
schooner began to leak. Captain Lock
hart could r.ot make San Francisco again,
but was driven down to San Diego, where
the Helen now is awaiting an overhauling
and a change of weather.

Calif ornians In Washington.
Several of the largest sugar refineries in Louisiana
Ere reported to have arranged for using Texas oil
instead of coal for fuel, and according to the esti
mates made at present prices $50,000 worth of oil will
do as much work as $150,000 .worth of coal."
In commenting upon the absorption of the Glas
gow Mail by the Record of that city, the Westminster
Gazette says the number of morning papers in Scot
land has been thus reduced to seven. Even so large
a city as Glasgow has but two, and Edinburgh only
one. ' â¢ ,
The persistent agitation which is now going on in
Kansas, Nebraska and Missouri for the formation of
a third party looks very much as if Bryan were pre
paring a place for himself in case the Democratic
reorganizes should squeeze him out of that party in
the next Presidential campaign.
The American officer detailed to study the South
African war reports that the Boers are not soldiers.
Considering what they have done, one may ask what
would have happened had they been soldiers? It
is worth while to remember that European officers
made the same comment on both the American armies
in our Civil War; yet it is probable that those armies
combined could have beaten the rest of the world in
arms.

PERSONAL MENTION.
Here, then, are two excellent thingsâ the suppres-;
sion of the" child labor system and an increase of the
public school system. Such movements will go fair
toward preserving the South from the reactionary
tendencies; of Bourbonism. They will help /to place
the social organism of the ,South _on the '"same", level
with that of the rest of the country,: and- when' that
occurs Bourbonism^ in politics . will .; vanish. ./ Indeed/
there arc already evidences of ; revolt against ?it,' and
the South may be said to be irnprovingali along the
The rule is to go Into effect: September 1. The as
sociation, it-is jinnounced, 'will urge* the Legislature
to increase 'the ; school facilities for the children of
the State, andthe members who represent the largest
arhount of property have ' declared a willingness to
bear the increase of taxation necessary to provide- a
long term of effective public school instruction.
. . According to the report of the proceedings, of the
association given, by the , Atlanta Constitution the
agreement recites "that the extended employment of
children growing out' of .local conditions . was riot
profitable, as the . leaks rnore than counterbalanced
the difference in wages that would be demanded by
more mature help; that hereafter under no circum
stances will children of 10 or under be employed; that
no child 'under 12 shall be employed at "night, save
in ease^ra widowed mother to whose support the
work rnay.be necessary, and that even this labor'will
notibe employed unless;it has four months' schooling*
each year."' ' Â¦ .' - ':, ' â¢ ! Â¦â
One of the: latest of these better . developments 13
the action taken by the _Georgia Industrial!Associa
tion to bring about the abolition of. ichildlabor.; The
issue has been under discussion a long tirne.V'In'fact,
a year ago the Georgia Legislature came very near
enacting, a stringent law forbidding such labor.' At
that time the mill-owners were opposed to the law,
but they have "since come Â¦ to the' conclusion to do
voluntarily what they objected to being forced to do.
WHILE'the actions of Alabama "and "Virginia
in following the lead of in- dis
franchising the negro show that Bourbon
ism is still the dominant element in Southern politics,
there are evidences that better elements are at work
in the social organism, and that they are greatly im
proving the- conditions ' of the Southern; people. ?
THE IMPROVING SOUTH.
A notorious gambler and a discredited. ex-Chief of
Police'of Seattle mixed things the othenday, and one
is dead and the other is in jail charged. with his mur
der. The pea'ce abiding citizens of the town are to be
congratulated -upon this improvement in. their con
ditions. â¢ Â¦Â¦'Â¦Â¦ '- ' .
The subject is' Qne that deserves the. earnest atten
tion of educators in mapping out the/courses of in
struction to be given in the schools at the opening
of the coming term. We are collecting at. consid
erable expense of time and money a museum 6f com
mercial exhibits, and it will be nothing more than
common, sense to fit young men and young women
to use it to the iftst advantage for" their employers.
The University of California is well, equipped to
take the lead in raising the study of geography in Cali
fornia to the ', position it holds in Gefmany. The
influence radiating from -Berkeley would soon have
good effects on ; the public schools and colleges of
the State, and especially in this city, where geo
graphical knowledge is so rhuch in demand. Were
such instruction given, bur merchants would soon
be able to obtain in their, employ young men who
could make a much more profitable use of the Com
mercial Museum than others who lacked. the knowl-.
edge which comprehensive geography teaches. ' . .
Now that we have learned so profitable a lesson
from the East we might well endeavor to. make it
more profitable still by learning in connection with it
a lesson from the thoroughness with which the Ger
mans teach geography. The rapid advance of the
Germans in the commercial world, which is one of
the most remarkable features of the history of the last
twenty-five years, has been the result of many forces
working harmoniously together. Not the least among
them is the stimulus to foreign, commerce given. to
young men in the schools and in the universities by
instruction in geography in the fullest sense of th'e
word. Such instruction docs not consist. wholly in
physical geography, or political geography ;# riot in
teaching the student how to locate on a map a river
or a' town, but it embraces the whole relation of man
to the earth, deals with everything of industry; com
merce and politic^ and, indeed, with well nigh the
whole of life. â¢*â¢>â¢'*'. ?.'\$%i ''â¢;*'' ?Â¦
With that much accomplished or undertaken it is
fair to assume that within a comparatively short time
we shall have a commercial museum second only to
the great institution in Philadelphia. That it will be
widely beneficial there is no reason to doubt. The
history of the museum at Philadelphia is conclusive
upon that point, for it reveals the fact that there is
an urgent demand on the part of manufacturers and.
merchants for just such exhibits as the museum af
fords. The later and larger developments of the mu
seum have, in fact, been- caused by efforts fb meet the
demands of trade. and industry, and have been in no
sense an extension or expansion of the enterprise from
a mere desire for bigness. , Trade conditions on the
Pacific are not so widely different from those on the
Atlantic but that we may with reason expect the his
tory of the museum at Philadelphia to be paralleled
by that of the museum in this city. . W
GRATIFYING reports have been received by
the officials of the Pacific Commercial Mu
seum of the progress made in the work of col
lecting exhibits. The agent in Manila of the museum
gives assurance of an excellent collection of articles
of trade and industry from the Philippines, the ex
hibit of Costa Rica at the Pan-American exposition
is to be obtained for permanent exhibition here, and
instructions have been prepared to direct the work
of an agent in collecting exhibits from the various
countries of South America.
TRADE AND, GEOGRAPHY.
The need for the service. is great,* and the impor
tance of it is well understood by everybody. It is
therefore gratifying that Secretary Wilson has so
promptly given the Weather Bureau authority to
install a wireless sj'stem as soon as a suitable one has
been found. The system found suitable on the Nan
tucket lightship will certainly be suitable on the Faral
lones. It is therefore to be expected that action will
be taken to establish the service here as soon as it has
been successfully in operation there. ' Certainly there
should be no delay. There "is no telling what day
some vessel lying in the fogs off the Farallones may
be ia dire need of just such service as wireless teleg
raphy could give. It may be safely said that in a
matter of this kind every day is of value. Immediate
action is therefore in the highest degree desirable,
and it is to be hoped the Weather Bureau officials
will be prompt to make use of the authority granted
them by the Secretary.
We have, then, the prospect of an early establish
ment of the much desired service between San Fran
cisco and the islands which, lying thirty miles away
at sea, serve as an outpost from which information
can be dispatched of the approach of shipping, and
also from which news can be sent to the Weather Bu
reau of all the phenomena which are useful to' the ex
perts of the bureau in making forecasts of the weather.
The Herald" plant is to go into operation July 1.
There may possibly be some defect in the machinery
that will disappoint expectations at the first attempt,
but of the eventual success of the service there are no
doubts in the minds of those who have given any at
tention to the work which has been accomplished by
the Marconi system. The Herald itself, for the pur
pose of completing the tests of the system, made the
recent experiments of sending and receiving wireless
messages between various stations on the British
coast and the steamer Lucania as she made her way
down the channel to the open sea, and they were en
tirely successful.
SAN FRANCISCO'S interest in the approaching
operation of the wireless telegraph service
which the New York Herald has installed on
Nantucket lightship is hardly less than that of New
York itself. Thanks to Secretary Wilson, it is well
assured that as soon as possible after the successful
operation of the plant on the Nantucket lightship the
Weather Bureau will install a similar plant for the
purpose of establishing wireless communication be
tween the Farallones and this city.
THE NANTUOKBT TELEGRAPH.
At one of the meetings of the Y. M. C. A. con
ference in 'Boston there was read a message from
King Edward expressing sympathy with the work of
the association. In describing what followed the
Boston Journal said: "Everybody shouted, and those
who had not climbed. upon their chairs stamped and
jumped up and down in a kind q! frenzy, and the less
demonstrative, whose hands were not busy waving
something, applauded in more conventional way. The
vast audience, larger ..than at any previous session of
the convention; and more brilliant, perhaps, for it
was a so_rt of college night, had sprung to its feet
at the mention of King Edward's name. A moment
of roaring enthusiasm, and some quavering voice
started 'God Save the King.'" Evidently the boys
had been jubilating with too much Boston lemonade.
CALIFORNIANS Â¦ may- again congratulate
themselves- thafthey;live in a .climate where
heat arid humidity' are not associated. In our
rainy season'it'is not : hot, and in our -hot season it is
not humid. Heat and â¢* humidity are .'.-hunting ' in
couples just novy and slaying people by scores from
the Rocky Mountains to the Atlantic! Coast' Drivers
fall dead from their seats ; and horses 'drop iri harness.'
The public hospitals are crowded ; with patients who
are prostrated by heat and humidity.
When the temperature* ran- from 92 at St. Paul arid
03 at Chicago to 102 at El /Paso, and the humidity
from. 70 to 90 , i$er cent, the maximum temperature
in California ,was 82 at Red Bluff, 76 at Sacramento,
84 at Fresno, 60 at San Francisco, 76 at Los Angeles,
and 64 at San; Diego; and the minimum temperature
at Red-Bluff was 60, at Sacramento 52, Fresno 52, San
Francisco 48, Los Angeles 58, and San Diego 60. A
little fire in open grates and fireplaces was found com
fortable, morning.and evening. The humidity in Cali
fornia was not worth recording. "
. . A study.of the Eastern heat and moisture discloses
the fact that the tropical isotherm runs into that af
flicted country in the summer, in respect both to heat
and humidity.' 'That same isotherm reaches far north
in California/but brings dry heat only, leaving its
moisture behind/ For an outsider to comprehend our
climatic advaritagesjit is necessary that he understand
this relation of Jieat and humidity. . â¢ ' ?Â¦' .
( If the country west- of' the \Rocky Mountains were
subject to a temperature . of go or 95, and an average
humidity of 85 for the greater part of the year, Â¦ it
would be uninhabitable to white men. Even now they
flee from the weather as from a pestilence, and by hun
dreds of thousands take refuge in far Northern re
sorts, on Lake Superior, the coast of Maine and in
the maritime provinces of Canada, while we are en
joying a little fire on the California hearth, or are at
work in a dry air at a temperature that would kill
thousands if associated with humidity.
In discussing tropical labor, the San Francisco
Chronicle says: fcWe do not believe it to be true
that white men cannot' labor in. the tropics. If is no
hotter there than in 'the. interior valleys of California."
The Chronicle might have.said truly that it is often
not as hot White men work in comfort in a Califor
nia dry temperature of 105, but the same men would
die in an hour in that temperature in Cuba, the Phil
ippines or Chicago. If the Chronicle thinks that trop
ical temperature and humidity are favorable to white
labor, why does it not praise this Eastern weather as
the best part of the year in that region, and advise
a rush thither from California? These insufferable,
conditions of heat and humidity in the Eastern part
of our own country, which lasf but a few weeks and
are intermittent, are the permanent conditions of the
tropics. People in this country are deceived by. the
report of temperature from Hawaii .and the Philip
pines. The maximum does not seem high, andVitHe 1
Chronicle helps the people to fly to conclusions* that
are not warranted by the facts. . A " â¢ ;'* '
Just fancy the conditions that, prostrated Chicago'
on the 24th and 25th inst. prevalent the whole year
with but slight intermission, and you have the con
ditions under which, the Chronicle insists that white
men can work in the tropics.
HEAT -^ND: HUMIDITY.
LOOKED at from the standpoint of availability for active
. service, the only true way to Estimate the efficiency of
the National Guard, Camp Gage was a success. Those
. , that went under the. tents last week in search of work
and .Instruction found both in, plenty. . Those that went In
search of play found but little, of it, and they have returned
complaining that the camp ground is too far from town, that
it was too big and that too little attention was paid to the
Instruction of the individual soldier..-.
It is true thafbut little time was spent upon the individual^
soldier, comparatively speaking, but 'the last day of the en-.
â¢campment showed. such a marked improvement in his knowl
edge and bearing that It was plain the "camp had supplied
many things in which he was lacking. Besides a soldier in an
army is a small thing. ' He is not even. a unit In the system un
til he has other men with him to forma squad, and since the
double rank formation was, adopted he IS only half as impor
tant as he was before. /In the guard, however, the individual
soldier seems to be paramount- He elects his officers and he
threatens them with failure of re-election if they go not his
way r in-;all things,- and' altogether the individual soldier has
much more consideration so far as his opinions, are concerned
than. he is entitled* to. The encampment was not for him as
an individual. It was for the soldier collectively, and particu
larly for his officers. Anything, in , the way of individual in
struction, if properly attended to, could have been taught In
1 the "armory before going â¢ into the field, and the camp could
then - serve its real > purpose â field Instruction. A few weeks'
drilling will make a'' good" soldier '.in all but experience, but it
takes years to make a good officer; and if the camp did no
more it demonstrated that the guard Is in need of good officers.
Real Discipline Lacking. .
The officers of the line maintained a fair discipline all
through the week, but it was not' the true discipline; not the
discipline and respect that comes for the. rank" and the straps
"regardless of the man; not the sort that prevented officers in
uniform from drinking and hobnobbing with men In uniform;
not the sort that made it Impossible for an order to be ques
tioned;^ not the sort that showed judgment In the handling
of the men and that- secured prompt obedience. The relations
between the. junior officers of the National Guard and the men
in the ranks are from a military standpoint entirely false, and
it is in' just those relations that discipline commences.
It Is' apparent there is nothing in the shape of Â«' man so
utterly helpless as a private soldier whose officers will not or *
cannot look out for him, and the encampment demonstrated
that should active service come there are many men who
would be in just that helpless condition. It is more important
to them if they are going to remain in the service that their .
officers be well trained than. that they themselves shall be well
trained, and such training can best be had In a large encamp
ment. The division camp developed the resources of the
Junior officers by giving them the duties that belonged to them
with only directory and not supervisory authority over them..
Every afternoon was left to the regiments to do as they
pleased. . Some company commanders used j the time In the
school of the squad and the school of the company; most of
them allowed their men to call It a half holiday with unlimited
passes. Yet they 'are the ones who are complaining that too
little was done for the individual, soldier. In a division en
campment the instruction of the. soldier belongs to the Junior
officers and to the sergeants If 'they be as. capable as they
should be. Field and general officers should have no time for
fours right and company lines. The Instruction of a sentry
belongs to the sergeant of the guard, and it can be done and
should be done by him. The camp demonstrated that fre
quently the instruction of a sentry was not beneath the duties
of a colonel.
It Is not the intention of the State that an encampment
shall furnish the school for the elementary instruction of sen
tries, of non-commissioned officers, of awkward squads and of
the dozen or two dozen other branches of the military art that
competent company officers can teach their men In their
armories, but it does contemplate the finish" of an armory edu
cation in the art of war. It expects that there shall be open
orde^, firing drills, skirmish lines of attack and defense, battle
rushes "and formations, the placing- of troops for assault and
to resist assault and all the points that governithe handling of
the men in the field and works for the purpose' of their organi
zation. It contemplates the teaching of the care of the men
in camp, their rations and their protection from disease within
as well as from assault, without, and the demonstration, sadly
necessary in the California Guard, that prompt and unques
tioning obedience, without any knowledge of the reasons for
it;' are the first attributes of a soldier, be he in the ranks or
bearing shoulder 'straps. With all this to : be crowded Into a
week. the Instruction of the individual soldier is a small ques
tion. He is to bÂ» considered only in his collective capacity.
; A Few of the Breaks.
An Instance of -this willingness on 'the part of Juniors to
take, charge of situations themselves was in the departure of
the First Infantry and the artillery battalion from this city.
The orders were that, the artillery should follow*he Infantry,
as It does rn all cases, even when the commanding officers are
of equal rank, but the artillery, started first from this city and
arrived first in camp. And the same thing happened at. the
breaking up. It may be urged that the artillery was ready
first, 7 but that has nothing to 'do with the situation. 'Again, to
go farther up the line, but one of the ; brigadier generals re
ported himself ready to leave the camp on the break-up. When
they were, ready they got out, leaving -the .time an indefinite
quantity until the commands started. And In smaller things â
It .was the custom for the "first few mornings for each brigade
to pipe up reveille and rout out its bands whenever the brig
ade timekeeper found the hour had arrived. There should not
have been a note until the call. from the guardhouse and the
raising" of *the-flagf..at-divlsiqn headquarters. There was noth
ing serious about any of those" and the many more little breaks
that occurred, but It showed plainly the desire to "go It alone 1 '
that. is. not part of a good military system. A right assumed
has : limits more or less indefinite, and it may.be exercised in
things Important as well as in things trivial. * In an 'enemy's
country it might easily become advisable that all calls be dis
pensed with. 'A brigade reveille under those circumstances,
that did not wait for._ a | call from headquarters, might prove
welcome information to an f opposlng outpost. â¢'
; the drills in open order, and above all in the practice
marches, ' was where the great work of the" encampment was
done; but they were almost entirely, drills more for the officers
than the men. It. is' easy or comparatively so for a soldier to
learn'how to "fire at will," to fire aSlimlted number of rounds
or to fire in "volley," but it takes longer to teach his officer
when to order the fire at will, the limited number of rounds or
the volleys. In the meantime the soldier should be able to get

CHANCE TO SMILE
One more thing was demonstrated by the division encamp
ment tha> Is important. The matter of transportation should
be separate and distinct from the camp allowance. If it costs
a regiment $1000 to go into camp at Santa Cruz and but $100 to
camp but a few miles from home, the saving in transportation
is Its best argument against traveling to a division encamp
ment The camping allowance should not be drawn upon until
the regiments have pitched their tents and occupied them, nor
after they are struck and the regiment starts for home It
costs the regiments of the First and Third brigades twlco as
much to camp with the division as it does tho regiments of.
the Second Brigade, yet the allowances are the same. Money
saved on transportation means more to spend on equipment
so the element of geography becomes an important one It
would be better if the State transported the regiments to* the
ground and home again and allowed them cash only for their
subsistence while in camp and for their equipment.
So in the last division encampment the complaint that there
was not enough individual instruction for the soldier means
that the junior officers did not attend to their duties; It means,
too, that there was no time and no place to do the work that
should have been done in the armory; It ia a protest against
the fact that the camp was not one for recruits, but one for
soldiers. A division encampment means that all the organiza
tions m the guard shall be placed side by side In their true re
lations to one another and as parts of a whole In which the
identity of each Is lost . That many of both the officers and
men do not see It In that light does not alter the fact, for that
is what active service means, and unless the guard ia carried
along with active service as its end and aim It is a useless ap
pendage to the treasury. The encampment brought out forci
bly what was apparent before, that what the guard needs is a
better appreciation of the principles of the military system, a,
greater willingness to perform the duties of one rank without
seeking higher "authority without higher rank, and a greater
unwillingness to accept high rank or any rank without having
or seeking to acquire the knowledge necessary to support it.
Men have been elected as good fellows In the guard to com
mands that place them absolutely, should they be called into
active service, in charge of the safety and the lives of scores
if not hundreds of men, and tbey have hardly the military abil
ity to attend to. their own safety. There are many -wtio are
soldiers no deeper than their uniforms. ' '..
The tour of duty in Santa Crus went to show that the
sooner a center of authority is recognized In the National
Guard of this State the better. Quicker than anything else,
the division encampment showed that companies and bat
talions are, after all, but small things when they become part
of an army, and it is so they should be treated. It ia hard for
an officer who knows his duties to give way to one who he is
sure doe's not, but that Is the service, and unless there is a
willingness to do so the whole thing must fall. It Is hard for
a private in the ranks to' realize that after all his rifle is the
only thing about him of any Importance, and that It is really
more serious not to have it clean than not to be clean himself,
but the fact remains, and nowhere Is It better demonstrated
than In a divisionÂ»encampment. In a squad in the skirmish
line a man ia a man, but In a battle line stretching across three
miles of country a man is no more than a spot on the hillside.
It is then he begins to realize that his life Is In the hands of
those above him. and when as a national guardsman he has a
chance to elect hia officers he should remember that and elect
good ones. A tent in the open with. five men In It is a con
tplcuous thing, but in a division encampment it is but a patch
of canvas with two flies and a half of men under it. All this
to the man who is in the guard for fun Is disagreeable, but the
enthusiasts understand It and are satisfied. It Is the base of
the military principle that it should be so.
Competent Officers Necessary.
The quartermaster's department showed itself efficient ia
every way. Colonel Rlckon, the division quartermaster, was
called home the second day and the arduous duties of the de
partment fell upon Major J. TV. A. Off, quartermaster of the,
First Brigade. A better man could not have been selected.
Contracts were made in onÂ© and everything was done through
the_ division, the principal result tot which system will be a
large savins In the expense.
The commissary department was in the bands of lieuten
ant Colonel Huber, and It ran along without a hitch until one
day one of the regiments complained that it had not enough
to eat. The complaint was investigated and It was found that
of the day's ration of meat there was still enough for another
meal, when it should have been dealt out to companies to the
last 'pound. An overzealous commissary had tried to make a
day's ration go half again as far as the commissary depart
ment expected or wished, and his command went short in con
sequence. There was a lesson that was of vital importance to
the regiment, although It had no individual instruction to the
soldier worked Into It.
The signal corps men worked harder than any set In camp.
They strung wires and established communication between
headquarters all through the grounds, and . their work on thÂ©
practice marches made apparent the reason the Government
was so willing: to take them at the opening of the war.
Colonel McCarthy's field hospital was as well equipped aa
the best in the city, and the canvas walls in place of those
hard finished made up the greatest difference. The principal
trouble was a lack of patients, for the camp was an aggres
sively healthy one.
Division Staff Efficient.
It was in the departments of the Â« staff, however, that thÂ«
camp found Its greatest field for Instruction. The division staff
proved itself an efficient body of men,, partly because each man.
is skilled in the department to which he has- been assigned.
,but mainly because all are enthusiasts: but even as it was it
was a valuable experience to them and quite as Important to
the man in the ranks, who will reap the benefits of competence
at headquarters. Down the line, however, among. the Junior
staff otecers, there is need of many morÂ© division encamp
ments.
all the Individual Instruction he need3. unless he heeds not
what Is going on around him. He^can learn to look out for
himself within his own limits, learn to shoot straight and to
leaving planning to those above him.
When Last's brigade, coming home from Its practice
march, appeared over the hill the call to arms was sounded
and within twenty minutes .the camp was guarded and out
posts were patrolling the country from a mile and a half to
two miles out. General Dickinson placed his men with a skill
and quickness that won comdliinent3 for him from the regular
officers who were attached to -the camp. In that hour of the
morning was one of the drills, for which camps are formed,
and the instruction of the individual soldier .was of secondary
importance while it was going on.
If the wild assertions of sensational journals' are
to be accepted the staid and "highly respected old
town, of Philadelphia can give the other cities of the
Union up-to-date lessons arid a little' more on muni
cipal corruption and official 'dishonesty, in everything
affecting the public welfare. -Perhaps the old town is
tired of being considered behind vthe times.
The death of the wife of President McKinley's Sec
retary of the Treasury and that of the son of his Secre
tary of State make his official family share the com
mon lot and universal fate.
When the attention is fixed upon such a group. as
a President and his Cabinet the havoc made among
all by the incidents of mortality is more plainly real
ized than when man is contemplated in the mass. In
Mr. Cleveland's first administration Secretary of State*
Bayard lost a loved daughter, Vice President Hen
dricks died, and a member of Attorney General Gar
land^ family passed away. Secretary Lamar did not
long survive his advancement to the Supreme bench.
In General Harrison's administration sorrow invaded
the White House in the death of" Mrs. Harrison, and
Blaine passed from the State Department to his grave.
Â¦And in Cleveland's second Cabinet Secretary of State
Gresham died.
Our people, who so recently .met the "bereaved
father, and admired him so sincerely, offer their con
dolence in an effusion of unaffected feeling..
Himself associated witlf the highest tragedy in our
national history, as the official confidant of Mr. Lin
coln in the Civil .Wary the Secretary was gratified by
the quality shown by this son,' who is dead untimely.
Mistakes might have been forgiven to his youth,
but he made none, and when his service was
over, with a sobriety of mind not often found at his
age he treated as a matter of course that he should
have done his duty, as that was expected of him. His
father is a man of peculiar goodness and simplicity of
life and manner, devoted to his family and of the
most lovable personal traits. This son was like all
his children, dear, and he felt great pride in the fault
less and honorable manner in which he had carried a
great responsibility.
.. How false and mistaken it all was is now history.
He reached his post and served his country with the
greatest activity, intelligence and wisdom. It is riot
easy to please a people under such awful stress as
w.ere the Boers. But he accomplished that delicate
and difficult task, and when the city of his post sur
rendered came home with the good will and praise
of all with whom his official duty brought him in con
tact, and at last with the praise of his own countrymen.
The young man -was sent to represent his country
as Consul to the Boer republic, and many there were
who delighted in deriding the appointment and in
predicting his failure to impartially represent our in
terests in that storm center of the world. He was ac
cused of pro-English sympathies, and a storm of
abuse followed his departure to duty that was fiercer
than the equinoctial gales that buffeted his ship.
THE whole country in a general and sincere way,
and his many friends in a very special and per
sonal way, will genuinely sympathize with Sec
retary. Hay in the tragic death of his very promising
son. . , .

IKEA. T. Vogelsang of the Board of Fish
Commissioners yesterday arrested a vio
lator of the quail law at Cloverdale. The
culprit was convicted and fined $40. Vio
lations of the game laws are becoming too
numerous to suit the commission, and. a
vigorous campaign against the lawbreak
ers Is planned. ' -
Game Law Violator Caught.
Frederick Ambich, an old newspaper
peddler, was found dead in his bed yester
day morning-. He had been sick for sev
eral days, and the autopsy showed that
death was due to cirrhosis of the liver.
Ambich was a familiar figure at the in
tersection of Market and Kearny streets
for many years. . \*L"
Death of Old Newspaper Vendor.

AN1> TRIES. TO LEAVE
Witness Aigainst Lookout Lawbreak
ers Attempts to Escape Through
Writ of Habsas Corpus.
Spencer & Raker, attorneys for A. C.
Colburn, a resident of Modoc County, who
gave . damaging testimony before the
Grand Jury In regard to the recent lynch
ing at Lookout, and who was placed In
confinement last Tuesday when it was
discovered that he intended to leave the
locality, filed a petition for a writ of ha
beas corpus in the Supreme Court yester
day.
â¢ Colburn knows a great deal about the
crime at Lookout, and was forced to tes
tify a week or more ago. His evidence
was ' of a highly damaging nature to the
accused parties, and he was warned that
any delay in that section of the State
might.cost him his life.
Colburn immediately settled up his af
fairs and was on the eve of departure
Â¦when his arrest was effected by Superior
Judge Harrington and District Attorney
Bonner. It is claimed by his attorneys
that his detention is illegal, and his im
mediate liberation is demanded.
- Between 4:30 and 6 p. m. dally Station D
holds the hardest working men in San
Francisco. They have thousands of let
ters to handle and are oppressed with the
certainty that, work as 'hard as they can,
they will never get all of the epistles into
the mails Tor which they are intended. It
is. not their fault, but the fault of the
merchants who delay until the last min
ute, or of clerks who fail to deposit let
ters earlier* in the day. With the in
crease of the mail the trouble has been
aggravated constantly and the Post
master, found It necessary to administer
the rebuke contained in the foregoing ex
tract from- his open letter. Within an
hour and a half, beginning at 4:30, the
Southern Overland, the Central Overland,
the "Owl" ;and the Coast Line mails all
get away, and they are all heavy with
thousands. of letters. â¢;â¢ r .Â¦Â¦..â¢â¢; V â¢
The prompt dispatch of a mall is a matter
which equally interests you and the PostofflcÂ©
Department. To insure .this It Is necessary that
mail should bo deposited in the- letter boxes
early and often. " The practice of . many mer
chants of permitting the mail of the day to ac
cumulate until the very last hour and then
depositing It in a bunch at the end off -the busi
ness day is the cause of much of the Irregular
ity- and delay complained of.
Â¦-: The merchants of â¢ San Francisco have
been admonished by Postmaster Mon
tague. Yesterday every merchant in the
city who has a large amount of mail mat
ter to handle received a small letter writ
ten In red Ink, In which he was forcibly
reminded of his or some other man's
shortcomings. Two hours later a score
of, men in Station, D of .the postofflce. at
t% foot v of Market street, perspiring as
they ruefully, gazed at tons of letters that
were dumped in for them to sort and get
away on malls about to depart, were won
dering whether the public would take the
advice of the Postmaster or would con
tinue.,,their heart-breaking practices of
the present. Postmaster Montague wrote:
Postmaster AdmonlShes
Them to Mail Letters
TSarlier.

Max H. Kershow, one of the best
known college athletes who ever visited
the Pacific Coast, passed away In this
city yesterday after a prolonged illness,
aged 29 years. His remains will be shipped
to an uncle in Philadelphia, his former
home, to-morrow. His parents are. dead.
Kershow was an extremely popular
man. He came to this city two years ago
and took up his residence here. He had
been ailing for years, and three months
ago was compelled to seek his bed. . He
was attended by Drs. Wagner and Renz.
At an early hour yesterday morning he
passed away. His brother Carl, Miss Ada
E. Thall his physicians and attendants
were at his bedside when the end came. â¢.
Kerahow was a graduate of Yale. - On
completing his studies at that Institution
of learning he entered. the Harvard Law
School. While at Yale he took an active
Interest In athletics and became ezoert la

The programme was a' long one and but
little time was lost after the guests had
arrived in getting it started. - Fred S.
Myrtle, president of the Press Club, ad
vanced to the footlights and welcomed
the editors and their friends from Ala
bama heartily and sincerely and in the
name of the club, the, newspapers of the
city and the California Press Association,
under whose auspices the guests were be
ing received in the clubrooms. He called
upon W. D. Pennycook, president of the
California Press 'Association, and he
echoed the words already spoken and
added the hope that the Alabamans would
come again and stay longer, if not perma
nently. W. E. Yerby,. chairman of the
delegation, expressed: the thanks of his
companions and' their appreciation of the
courtesies shown them. \ . -
Then, as* Myrtle put it, v the serious part
of the programme was taken up. There
was a piano duet by Charles 0. Henley
and Ray Irwin, which was encored
George King sang ".What of the Bow "
and then In response to Insistent applause
"The Bedouin's Love Song."; Miss Rose
Broderick sang. two pretty little soprano
songs, and Homer Henley followed with
a ringing barytone solo that kept palms
beating until he sang twice more.- Miss
Etta O'Brien . followed, and after her Dan
Lawrence, and then the ever entertaining
Billy Hynes came out to sing and "crack
Successful Programme.
vaded by an atmosphere of friendliness
and good fellowship ever present when
newspaper men meet newspaper men and
sit down to enjoy a pipe and a bowl to
gether.
The assembly hall had been rigged in
its prettiest for the occasion. Across the
stage hung- the word "Alabama" in let
ters of white. Above it was a shield bear-
Ing ' the crest of Alabama, and on each
side -was looped up' bunting of red and
white, its folds caught in bunches of pink
roses and a confusion of green. From
the center of the hall hung bunting of
red, white and blue, with streamers of
red radiating over the ceiling and ending
in electric lights, and pendent from these
were big bunches of ferns. Around the
centerpiece was set a square of Japanese
lanterns. Brilliantly hued and swinging
restlessly they colored the decorations
overhead like the turn of a kaleidoscope.
At the molding above each window a
shield flashed brightly from a nest* of
green, and from below it sprang great
green brakes "out and up among the
lights twinkling on the ceiling. Bunting
of red and white was looped along the
walls. More clusters of green branches
studded with roses like points of dull Are
completed the decorations on the walla
and windows, and among the clusters and
from under the bunting and over the ceil
ing the electric lights glittered and flashed
as if they, too, were inspired . with the
fellowship- of the occasion.

The Alabamans are looking forward
with pleasure to the excursion through the
Presidio and. Golden Gate Park to the Cliff
House, Sutro Heights and ocean beach.
The Pacific Ocean is what they are most
Interested In, as many of the party dwell
Inland.
Mr. Pennycook of the Vallejo Chronicle
represented the California Press Associa
tion. Mr. Harrison of the San Francisco
Press Club and a committee of ladies con
sisting of Mrs. Hugh M. Burke, Mrs. Ja,r
nest C. Stock and Miss Marie Lawler con
tributed to the pleasure of the guests
aboard the Defiance.
The cruise was not extended to Mare Is
land and Vallejo, because the visitors did
not arrive In San Francisco until 10 p. m.
Wednesday evening and are scheduled to
depart Saturday morning. It was deemed
best to give them two hours of the after
noon to do a little shopping and see the
sights of the city In their own way.

For this afternoon The Call is endeavor

The resolutions had been passed and the
last of the refreshments were rapidly dis
appearing whim : Mr. Myrtle announced'
that The Call building, was to be lighted
up at 12 o'clock, and as the people crowd
ed'to the big windows of .the assembly
hall the tall tov.er flashed in the
darkness, outlined in fire and raising its
brilliant crest ! high over the surrounding
buildings until it seemed, to pierce the
sky. In ; the distance the thousands of
lamps blended their rays until the faces
of the building glowed like heated iron
and the tall structure < stood out against
the sky like a new -sun" in the heavens.
For an hour it flashed its* radiance into
the night, and then suddenly as it came
it went again, leaving only â¢ the single
spark that always burns bright on the
crest of its lofty dome.

NEW YORK. Jun* 27. â There waÂ« quit* a
lively spilling out of stocks for a time to-day
sf a result of the suspension of the Seventh
National Bank, one of the members of the
Ckarins-house Association-, Jh consequence of
action by the Comptroller of thÂ« Currency,
iiut the market at no time approached any
where near to demoralisation. The drop In
prices was, howevÂ«r, very general and ex
tended to over S point's ta a number of the
principal active stocks which have been re
cent Javorites in the speculation. St. Paul,
I'nicn Pacific and Manhattan were the severest
pufferere and Missouri Pacific and Texas and
Pacific also yielded rather easily. The latter
part of the day. howwer, witnessed a striking
an<\ general recovery, which practically wiped
oat the earlier losses and established consid
<;aMe gains fcr some leading stocks. Union
Pacific. St. Paul Â¦.Â«<} Metropolitan Street Rail
v.-jiy were the only etoclcs among -the leaders
\vh:ch failed to benefit fully from this, late
