<vA thÂ»ir buying to cover had considerable to
<".ri uith the late rally. There was an opinion'
i>rrva.lÂ«nt that rorae of the late financial in
terests were quietly Â¦ taking advantage of the
low level of prices to pick up stocks, without
making any attemp to Eupport prices. But
this opinion is hardly consistent with the gin
. Cfrly manner in which prices were bid up after
the- selling pressure had relaxed, with a palp
able disinclination to secure stocks. The sell-
Ing, for London account, which was supposed
to originate in Berlin, was aÂ» factor of con
siderable importance, which was probably not
sufficiently .allowed for in current estimates
of the market. The reports of further banking
troubles in. Berlin did not seem to have any
striking effect on sentiment here. The suspen
sion of the Seventh National Bank came as a
surprise, as it 'was generally supposed' that
friends of the bank would supply sufficient re
sources to carry it*through. The worst effects
of the embarrassment had been discounted on
Tuesday, during the period of suspicious con
jecture, when rumors were rife of far-extended
banking clifficulties. The disclosures since
made of the bank's affairs and of the charac
ter of the securities which had Involved it in
its difficulties served to give come confidence
thnt the trouble was localized, as these securi
ties were not dealt in on the exchange and
are not widely distributed. The Incident was
found, nevertheless, to create an unpleasant
Impression and serve^j to Intensify the pinch
in the money market incident to jhe turn of
the half year.
There was considerable shifting of loans,
partly in preparation for next week's dividend
disbursement and partly on account of throw.
!ng out of* unsatisfactory collateral for call
loons. The call ican rate ran up to 15 per
rent. With this substantial recuperation In
prices of standard securities since . the Â¦ May
panic the fact has rather been lost sight of
that euch a period of speculative confidence
and inflation as culminated then is bound to
leave its residue of doubtful securities which
might be easily marketable in a period 'of ex
pansive confidence, but would be a drug on the
market in quieter times. The market closed
very dull but firm at the recovery. There was
tsomt selling of railroad bonds in sympathy
withVtocks. Total sales, par value, $2,025,000.
I.'nited States bonds were all unchanged on

er. denouncing "hoodlumism and lawless

mandant of marines, already has been

ALAMEDA, June 27.â With grappling
irons husband and son dragged the waters
of the tidal canal this, morning in the
search for the body of the -wife and
mother, Mrs. Caroline Morgenroth, who
disappeared from her home, 2511 Santa
Clara avenue, Tuesday morning, after re
peated threats to end her life and suffer
ings After a two hours' search, rowing
up and down in a rowboat, the efforts of
the anxious relatives of the unfortunate
woman were rewarded by the discovery
of the body, the grappling hooks catching
in the clothing and bringing It to the sur
face..
Mrs. - Morgenroth i left J her home at ?
o'clock Tuesday morning, escaping the
vigilance of her family, to whom she had
often said that If she lived near the. tidal
canal she would Jump into the waters and
end her sufferings, of long standing. Her
daughter was awakened by the striking of
a clock, when she discovered that the
front door was wide open.
The daughter knew that her mother had
escaped from the house and alarmed the
family. All night they searched for her
without finding atrace. except footsteps
leading out of the yard. When daylight
broke the search was kept up and contin
ued until this morning.
Mrs. Morgeni*oth was a native of Alsace,
Germany, aged 55 years, and had resided
with her family in Alameda nineteen
years. She leaves a husband and five
children. '

I President Horace G. Burt of the
I Union Pacific arrived from
JJL Omaha, in his private car yester
day afternoon. He looked the typ
ical railroad president sitting in his car
as the train rolled Into the Oakland mole,
fanning himself and poring over charts
and maps that were laid out on a table 1
in front of him.
What is President Burt out here for?
He said yesterday that he was just tour
ing the coast for pleasure and that he
might go to Los Angeles to visit his sis
ter, who resides there. The railroad men
around the yellow building, however,
claim that this visit is a result of the
Harriman interests controlling the South
ern and Union Pacific, and that this is
the first of many consultations that will
be held between Presidents Hays and
Burt with a view to bringing the
roads Into closer affiliation. Hays and
Burt met in conference in Chicago about
two weeks ago, and it is presumed that
the arrival of Burt in San Francisco
means that they will talk over certain
matters that lack of time prevented dis
cussing at their last meeting.
President Burt would not talk about
railroad affairs. In fact, he said that he
only knew that J. C. Stubbs had been ap
pointed traffic director of his road through
the newspapers, and that he had not yet
received an official circular announcing
the appointment.

most conservative delegates propose be
sjdps the vote which every man has he
fhall also be allowed an extra vote if he

if he has a degree. In this way one man

the face of affairs. This movement will
be proposed as an offset to universal suf
frage.

PLATTSBURG. N Y., June _7.~JosÂ«ph
Ladue. founder of Dawson City. In the
Klondike, died last evening at his home
in Schuyler Falls. He had not been well
sÂ»nee his return from Alaska and spent
last winter at Colorado Springs in a vain
search for health. He fell m. victim to con
sumption, contracted in the severe north
ern climate. He leaves a wife and one
son.
Joseph Ladue was born on a farm near
the northern end of Lake Champlain.
Being of an adventurous nature, in his
rarly manhood he went to the far North
west and finally located on the Upper
Yukon, having been attracted there bv a
fine woodland in the neighborhood. Here
ht bought 1(W acres of land, built a saw
mill and established an embryo trading
J? oet - _, 11 wa * upon his land that gold was
discovered in the Yukon region, and
Laaue s trading: post became the prosper
ous city of Dawson. His esta/es in the
Klondike regnon. with the property that
ne nas sold, are said to be worth several
millions of dollars. Ladue returned to his
hcrne near Plattsburg in July. 1897. and
told strange stories of the sold-mad
Â£Olcny in the north. Love fftr Miss Anna
Mason of Schuyler Fulls. Ni Y.. led the
gold king back to civilization. She had
been engaged to Ladue for many years
and the marriage had been postponed
from time to time awaiting the day when
the lumber business on the Yukon should
justify their union. Fortune was the ally
of romance and Miss Mason became Mr*.
Ladue a few weeks after her fiance's re
trrr. in 1S97.

OAKLAND, Juiie 27.â "We, the jury in
the above entitled cause, find the defend
ant, W. A. Brandes, guilty of manslaugh
ter and recommend him to the mercy of
the court. . H. E. MOSHER, Foreman." v
William A. Brandes, once sentenced to
life imprisonment for the murder of his
daughter Lillian, smiled when he heard
the foregoing verdict ..read In Judge Og
den's court this noon. The decision of the*
twelve men who heard the evidence at
the. second trial of the child slayer was
a palpable- compromise. " Eight jurors had
voted for a verdict of murder, either in
the first degree or in the second degree.
One hard-headed juror had declared the
accused parent ought to be burned at the
stake, but after nearly twenty hours of
deliberation in the jury' room two men,
Thomas Agnew and W. H. Rogers, with
the assistance of Henry Hauschildt, a
Redwood Canyon farmer, turned the ver
dict into one of manslaughter, with the
rider of recommendation to mercy.
Brandes. is more than satisfied with the
result of this trial. In spite of his declar-'
atlons that he believed he , should have
been acquitted, the man was. overjoyed,
and he displayed his gratification in every
move.
Attorney A. L. Frick. who defended
Brandes, was content. He had very little
comment to make. , .
"From a- professional point of view I
consider the verdict, a decided victory, 1 '
he said, "but, of course, from the stand
of the defendant it is not." ..
"Hauschildt wanted some more instruc
tions from the court this morning, but
when he found Judge i Ogden could not
give them until late this af tern ion he de
cided to vote for provided
the recommendations to mercy, were
added." Â¦ Â¦ â¢
The crime for which Branded has been
twice convicted was the killing of Lil
lian A. Brandes at Berkeley in Novem
ber, 1898. Her body was found hanging to
a bedpost, with external evidence of sui
cide, which the law officers have always
contended was arranged by Brandes and
his wife to cover the murder. The. body
of the child when taken in charge by the
Coroner was found covered with bruises,
plain evidence of brutality. An autopsy,
conducted by Dr. S. H. Buteau and seven
surgeon' assistants, disclosed the entire
absence of any trace of stmngulation,
but, on the other hand, sufficient brain in
jury from blows was uncovered to war
rant the opinion that the girl had been
foully handled. Immediately after these
disclosures Brandes and his wife, Mrs.
Etta Brandes, were arrested. She was
the dead child's stepmother. After a long
trial Bxandes was convicted of murder in
the second degree, but Mrs. Brandes was
discharged. The defense fought their case
out on a battle of opinion between medi
cal experts, the theory being held to that
the girl had actually killed herself. .. â¢ '
That line was utterly abandoned at the
trial just concluded. Attorney Frick prac
tically admitted that the child had been
murdered, but that Mrs. Brandes and not
his client was responsible for her death.
The Brandes' son Milton testified at much
length about a fight between his mother
and his stepsister. His narrative was in
tended to convince the jury that Mrs.
Brandes had struck the girl with a piece
of hose to prevent the child from assault
ing her with a knife.
By other means all the effort of the de
fense was centered. in an attempt to fix
the blame upon the woman who had once
beÂ«n" accused, but nevev was'prosecuted,
either as a principal or as an accessory to
the death of the girl. Now Brandes' law?
yer* contends that Mrs. Â¦ Brandes- is be
yond the pale of the law, having been
discharged at the first trial.
Brandes was granted a new. trial by
the Supreme Court upon the same ground
that won Mrs. Botkln and Murderer Hoff
of San â¢ Francisco another- trial; namely,
error of the Judge In giving certain in
structions concerning circumstantial . ev
idence. Â¦
Brandes said to-day: â¢ "I feel : that 'I
should have been acquitted. At any rate,.
I am going to try to free myself. I shall
make a fight for a new trial."

TERRIBLE DEED
OF INCENDIARY
Farmhouse Set on Fire
Because Owner Defies
Blackmailers.
Wife of Farmer Is Fatally
Burned While Trying
to Save Papers.
TOPEKA, Kans., June 27.-Horribly
burned and dying in intense agony, was
the fate of Mrs. W. C. Carlson, wife of
a Cowley County farmer, because her
husband refused to deposit $5000 in a place
named by unknown blackmailers. Three
weeks ago Carlson, who is wealthy, re
ceived an anonymous note requesting
him to bury $5000 at the foot of a certain
telephone pole, under penalty of having
his house- burned. The suggestion was
ignored. One week later another anony
mous note was received stating that If
$5000 was not deposited within a week hi3
house would be burned and he would be
killed. No attention was paid to these
communications and on Saturday night,
while Carlson was en route to Medford
for mail the home was fired. Mrs. Carl
son was found about ten feet from the
ruins by Mr. Dunn, a neighbor, horribly
burned and insensible. She was taken
home by Dunn, where she rallied enough
before dying Sunday to relate the trans
action. She rushed outside when she dis
covered the fire, but returned for some
papers and a treasured old violin and
tried to save them. From that moment
her mind was a blank until she awoka in
the home of Dunn.

A benefit entertainment and dance for
Charles Es "Wright, a newsboy now ill at
St. Luke's Hospital, was given at Union
Square Hall last night by the Manhat
tans, a boys' social club. The attendance
was large and a considerable sum was
realized for the aid of the unfortunate
youth and his mother.
t .T, he Programme included a sketch by
Lillian Levy and Arthur Parent, female
impersonations by Melville Coakley, cor
net solos by Ruby Crosby, vocal solo by
William Sterns, specialties by Anita La
mont, a recitation by Elton Lambert,
tenor solo by Matt Keefe, monologue by
Henry Anbach and numbers by Charles
F. Graeber's mandolin and banjo orcnes
tra. . .
_ Th ,e committee in charge consisted of
Fred J. Forsyth, Edward A. Dehay, Wal
ter Cook, Ferd Younger, William Hath
away, Thomas McCormlck and Arthur
Moise. Edward A. Dehay and Fred J.
Forsyth comprising the floor committee.

It was feared that confusion would re
sult if so many thousand visitors should
attempt to register at headquarters dur
ing the two or three days previous to the
convention, and so far as possible the
work will be done on the trains before
they reach this city.
The canvassers for subscription funds
have collected nearly half of the $25,000
which is required, and it is expected that
the rest will soon be on hand.
"Dr. Filben. chairman of the general
committee, states that no difficulties are
being encountered in any branch, and
that all arrangements are progressing to
the satisfaction of those concerned.
Dr. Kingsbury, in charge of registra
tion, states that all members of Christian
Endeavor, Baptist Young- People's Union
and other societies of similar purpose will
be . entitled to all the privileges of the
convention. Delegates who register will
be supplied with the league badge, which
will give access to all meetings. Regis
tration will be by cards, which will be so
arranged that the convention address of
any delegate can be readily given. A
fipecial postofflce has been ararnged for,
and mail will be delivered to registered
visitors daily.
Special arrangements have been made
with the railroads and transfer companies
so that baggage will be handled carefully
and expeditiously. Every visitor who
gives notice of his coming: will be pro
vided with Quarters on satisfactory
terms. Guides and an information bu
reau will be at the disposal of all who
may wish to avail themselves of them. It
is a matter of the deepest concern to the
committees that no difficulties shall be
met by those who attend the convention
and no effort will be spared to Insure the
satisfaction of the visitors.
The plans for the reception and enter*
tainment of the many thousands of dele
gates who will come to this city for the
Epworth League Convention" arÂ© now
practically complete. The committees In
charge of the affair have profited from
their experience with the Christian En
deavor convention In 1897, and all the dif
ficulties and inconveniences which at
tended that event will be obviated.
Inconveniences of Former
Convention to Be
Obviated.
Delegates Are Assured
of a Pleasant.

EPWDRTH LEAGUE
PLANS COMPLETE
Thflu order was included in a circular
sent to the principals and teachers under
date. of March 21, 1901. It has all the au
thority of a positive order, and violation
of Its provisions will entail the same pen
alties as violations of orders of the board.
Despite the order complaints are' being:
made that the practice is still in vogue
in several of the schools. It is claimed
by parents that no true account can be
kept of the scholarship of a child if he
or she is to be lined credits won by a
knowledge of the course of study when
ever a lapse in deportment occurs. It is
claimed that the markings for ability and
the markings for deportment should be
separate, as the Superintendent has or
dered, and that if it be necessary to estab
lish a standard in deportment it should
be done by a system that does not en
croach on the pupil's ability to learn.
If the practice continues another circu
lar, and a stronger one, will be issued
which will deal with the subject.
I am authorized by the Board of Education
to state that no credits assigned for scholarship
should be deducted - from the record of a pupil
on account of poor deportment. Deportment
records and scholarship records must be kept
distinct and separate.
It Is some time since Superintendent of
Schools R. H." Webster ordered that In no
case should a teacher deduct' from the
scholarship of a pupil because of poor de
portment. It is an old custom, or it was
until the order was issued, ' that in ad
ditions to "checks" for bad behavior, a
teacher would deduct, "credits," or mark
one or three or live "failures" against the
obstreperous one, whether he failed in his
lessons or had them perfectly.
The injustice of the system was always
apparent, but It was not until â¢ the mat
ter was brought to the. attention of the
Superintendent by the parent of one of the
pupils that the following order was is
sued: : â¢ -
Be Abolished.
Custom of Deducting Credits
for Poor Deportment Must
Teachers May Not Re
duce a Scholar for (;
"CHECKS" HELD
TO BE ENOUGH

ADVERTISEMENTâ Office of the Chief En-
rlneer. Division of the Philippines. Manila.
P. ! I., March 1. 1901â Sealed proposals for
constructing a harbor at Manila, P. I., will
(a received at this office until 11 o'clock a.
m., August 1. 1901, and then publicly opened.
Work to be done Includes about 150.000 cubic
yards riprap. 21,000 cubic yards concrete and
rubble masonry In breakwaters about 6,000,-
000 cubic yards of dredging and a-plln bulk-
head 4700 feet long. Dredging in mud. sand
and shells to a depth of thirty feet. Dredged
materials to be uted for reclaiming land.
Bids must be accompanied by a bond of ona
hundred thousand dollars (1100,000) in gold.
One contract will be -made for the whole
work. Contractor's bond .will be 10 per cent
of the amount bid. The right is reserved to
rejecti any or all bids. For specifications and
blank forms applv to this office. Bureau of
Insular Affairs. Washington. D. C.; United
States Engineers Office* at T N * w fc r T Sflri^ l U'
ca*o and flan Francisco. JOHN DIDDLE.

expected that the combination will l>Â©
beneficial both to the public and the cor
porations.

Judge Sloss has appointed the Union
Trust , Company receiver of the money
and property now in the hands of Thomas
C. Jacqu'es; which it is alleged he secured
through the exercise of undue influence
from aged Joseph O'Hare. Jacques
through his attorneys. Knight & Hegger
ty and ex-Governor Budd, made a long
fight to prevent the appointment of a re
ceiver, but their efforts were without
avail. "

Dr. W. B. Coffey, chief surgeon of the
Market-street Railway Company, is a pa
tient at the Southern. .Pacific.. Bailroad
Hospital. He has been confined to his ,bed
for the last two days by a severe attack
of rheumatism. The physicians . at the.
hospital will not permit any one to see
him. as he must have absolute quiet.
Dr. Coffev has been ailing for the last
month, but has continued to attend his pa
tients. He was advised by friendsto take
a vacation, but- always put it off because
he was too busy. -Â¦' ,
Dr. Coffey Is a member of the Board
of Health and is prominent in social and
fraternal organizations. He has many
friends, and all regret that he is ill and
trust he will soon be convalescent.

decided to reopen the charter roll of the
union. This action was taken at the re

clusion ' of the regular business of the
meeting, the 'following officers were in
stalled: President, J. F. Keefe; vice pres
ident. Ed Hagemann; secretary and walk
ing delegate, John McCaffery; treasurer,
Jacob Foge.

The Electrical Workers will present a
new schedule of wages for the signature
of their employers after July 1. The new
schedule calls for an increase of 50 cents
per diem in wages.

At a meeting of the Building Trades
Council held last night a resolution was
adopted protesting against efforts to pre
vent the re-enactment of the Chinese ex
clusion act and calling upon the Senators
and Representatives to do all In their
power to secure this coast against Japa
nese immigration.

decrying the publication of certain arti
cles in Town Talk.

Dr. C. D. Balflcld will apply for a writ
of injunction to-day to prevent the Board
of Public Works, the Board of Education
and Xewsom & McNeil, contractors, from
proceeding with the work of improve
ments to the Dudley Stone School. The
contract was recently let for the erection
of a third story to the building at a cost
of J7979.
Associated with Dr. Salfield as plaintiffs
Jn thÂ« case will be J. P. Klncaid. W. B.
Hunt. H. Templcman and Mrs. Annie
Martin, residents in the vicinity of the
school.
The injunction will be applied for on
the ground that section C3, or No. 2927. of
the sreneral orders of the Board of Su
pervisors, limiting the height of frame
buildings to fifty-five feet is violated in
the contract, and that no ordinance grant
ing a special privilege was passed. The
further ground will be urged that the ad
jacent property will be injured in value by
the proposed improvement.
Taxpayers who are opposed to the
building of a third story on this primary
school claim that the building would be
come unsafe for young children. Thev
say further that the play gTOund is now
inadequate for the children, and that a
new site should be secured in a portion of
the district where children are now com
pelled to go too great a distance to reach
the. Dudley Stone School.

Buena Vista School. Commissioner Man
son of the Board- of Public "Works said
that the cost of the improvement will be
$11,000. The interior and exterior of the
structure are to be finished in the same
manner and of the same material and de-
EigT> as the present building. The board
also adopted plans for improvements to
the- Fremont School at an estimated cost
of â¢$1650.

San Francisco Is peculiarly situated in the
theatrical way. It costs money to bring
the best attractions out here and then a
constant change of faces is demanded, so
that in engaging people we can only offer
them a short season. But now we are so
placed that we will be able to offer long
engagements and at the same time keep
bringing out new people. Then another
thing. Take the scenic features, in an
elaborate production like 'Uncle Tom's
Cabin.' They entail a heavy outlay as
things now stand. With our chain of
theaters all can be shipped on to the other
houses and with very little expense. made
to serve in the production at them. At
Alcazar Theaters on the same lines as w<
have in the past.
"People want amusements and the
atrical entertainments at popular prices
and we have succeeded now In reaching a
position where we will be able to glvt
them the very latest and best in the
dramatic line at popular prices. We havt
also concluded arrangements with some
of the very best players In the country,
and you may rest assured that our firm
will hold no second place as purveyors ol
amusements."
Mr. Belasco says i the complete details
of the new arangement will be made
known soon. .
the Central Theater we have ample room
for the executive 'offices, painthouses
and supply warerooms, so that we can
prepare, equip and rehearse the organiza
tions sent out to the other theaters.
'"Robert Downing will be director gen
eral- of the productions put on at all the
theaters in our circuit, and will supervise
and organize the road companies which
will be sent out bv us. He Is a master in
such lines and that he has consented to
assume this trust Is an assurance that
our productions and people will be of the
very highest class. We have acquired
rights to the very best plays extant and
will- continue to conduct the Central and
